                            <div class="player-info-box-header "><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Michael Morse</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team">&nbsp;</div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 35</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: R/R</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-item">6' 5" / 245</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-pos">1B/OF</div></div>
                        <div class="player-info-bio">
                            <strong>Birthdate: </strong>3/22/1982 (35 y, 11 m, 16 d)<br /><b>Drafted:</b> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=3035&position=1B/OF#draft" style="text-decoration:none;">2000 June Amateur Draft - Round: 3, Pick: 12, Overall: 82, Team: Chicago White Sox</a><br /><strong>Contract:</strong> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=3035&position=1B/OF#contract" >$16M / 2 Years (2015 - 2016)</a>
                        </div>
                    </td>

(8/11/2017)<div class="spacer_5"></div><b>Injury:</b> Concussion, Day-to-Day</div></td></tr>

        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>One of the pleasant surprises on the 2010 Nationals, Morse flashed enough power -- 15 homers in just 266 AB -- to earn at least a part-time job in 2010. There's a good chance it was a career year, though. He turns 29 in March, and his .289/.352/.519 was well above his career minor-league .271/.330/.425. The batting average and OBP are particularly likely to come down, as they were likely inflated by a .330 BABIP. (His career BABIP is .348, but though he’s played in parts of six seasons, he’s barely amassed a season’s worth of plate appearances.) Depending on the Nyjer Morgan situation, he could see significant playing time in the Nats’ outfield and is likely to provide double-digit homers. But that’s about his only advantage in a 5x5 league: he doesn’t run, the Nats won’t give him many RBI or run-scoring opportunities, and his average is bound to come down. He shouldn’t be more than a waiver-wire pick-up for fantasy owners. (Alex Remington)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Pleasant surprise in 2010, and a possible source of waiver-wire power in 2011.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>As a lumbering 29-year-old with a pretty modest Minor League track record, Morse looked like a prime candidate to come back to earth after hitting .289/.352/.519 in 293 plate appearances in 2010. Instead, The Beast ravaged pitchers for a .303/.360/.550 triple-slash in 575 PA, cranking 31 home runs in the process while splitting time between first base and left field. The former White Sox and Mariners prospect was ultra-aggressive, chasing 38 percent of pitches thrown out of the strike zone and leading all National League hitters in first-pitch strike percentage, but his epic power allowed him to rock a .387 wOBA that ranked just outside the top 10 among MLB hitters. Morse isn’t a good bet to bat .300 again, considering his batting average on balls in play was .344, but he can’t be considered a fluke after hitting at an elite level over the past year and a half. Look for him to play first base more often in 2012 as the Nats tire of a one-armed Adam LaRoche and look to improve their team defense. (David Golebiewski)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>2011 was a career year for Morse and pitchers may try to use his free-swinging ways against him, but a .280/.330/.500-type year with 25 home runs isn’t out of the question. That’s plenty useful for a guy with multi-position eligibility. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Michael Morse attempted to prove that walks are overrated last season. Morse walked in just 3.7% of his plate appearances, which was one of the worst rates in the league. He still managed to hit for a high average and showed that his power wasn't a fluke. His free-swinging ways could come back to bite him at some point, but until that happens, he's a solid option in the outfield. He now has much less value in his new home -- not only is Safeco a pitcher's park, but he's surrounded by corner outfielders, first basemen and designated hitters. He'll probably emerge from the scrum with significant playing time, and the walls are being altered in Seattle, but the 31-year-old has seen his ceiling lowered with the trade to the Mariners. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Chris_Cwik">Chris Cwik</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Morse is capable of putting up solid numbers despite a terrible walk rate. Now with the Mariners, his role is up in the air, and his power numbers may suffer.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The reunion wasn't supposed to go this way. Michael Morse, jettisoned by the Seattle Mariners for Ryan Langerhans long ago, discovered his power stroke as a member of the Washington Nationals, once crushing 31 home runs and slashing .303/.360/.550. The Mariners, in desperate need of the mythical big bat, brought him back... and he started hitting like Ryan Langerhans. Through an injury-plagued 76 games, Morse hit .226/.283/.410 with 13 home runs all while playing a particularly miserable form of outfield defense. He was then traded to the Orioles, where he made 30 plate appearances and basically did absolutely nothing. He had arthroscopic surgery on his wrist a few months later. Morse would be best used by a team in need of a designated hitter, but don't tell the San Francisco Giants that, as they've committed to regular playing time in the field. Despite that, his fragility makes him a huge risk in fantasy baseball. If he could manage to stay healthy for 500 at-bats, it's possible he'd give you 25 home runs. He's an interesting flier in late rounds. (<a href="https://twitter.com/michaelcbarr">Michael Barr</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>If Morse could stay healthy, he'd be an interesting option as a final outfielder. He clearly has power, but he's only managed to stay off the trainers table consistently once in his career. There's reward with this risk, but you're probably best leaving his troubles to another manager.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Morse signed a two-year deal with the Marlins and figures to be their primary first baseman. Marlins Park obviously isn't an ideal spot for a power hitter, but it's not like Morse was in a favorable park in San Francisco, so it's a lateral move from a ballpark perspective. Morse should be a bump in playing time after failing to get more than 500 plate appearances in each of the last three seasons. With that much work, he could hit 20 home runs, and 25 if he gets some luck on fly balls. But you have to be concerned about his batting average. He's always maintained a higher-than-normal batting average on balls in play (career .333), but that wasn't the case in 2013. His strikeout rate also rose that year and he hit a paltry .215 as a result. His BABIP rebounded last year, but his strikeout rate remained elevated. If the BABIP falls again, he could have a disastrous batting average. It's not like he has a lot of success on balls in play due to an elite line drive rate or elite speed, so his BABIP seems a little tenuous. Draft him late if you need power, but be wary of that batting average. (<a href="https://twitter.com/TheRealTAL">Brett Talley</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Morse is moving from one bad ballpark for power hitters to another after signing with the Marlins. He can be a cheap source of power, but he's striking out too much in recent years and is a batting average risk. Proceed with caution.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>It seems like the Pirates have been looking for a first basemen for years now, and while prospect Josh Bell is the frontrunner to solve that problem in the future, it’s unclear whether he will be starting for the major league team on Opening Day.  If Bell does begin the year in the minors, then the Pirates seem prepared to enter the 2016 season with a platoon of the left-handed John Jaso and right-handed Michael Morse at the position.  Morse was bad in limited plate appearances in 2015, but when he last enjoyed extended playing time in 2014, he produced a 138 wRC+ versus left-handed pitchers with eight home runs in 155 plate appearances.  Still, you can’t just ignore the 83 wRC+ season Morse had last year, and with Bell and intriguing sleeper Jason Rogers as possible alternative first base options as soon the start of the season, it’s risk to draft Morse and Jaso and assume you’ll have an everyday player by combining them.  Morse’s best-case scenario ends in no more than 300 plate appearances.  Even with his decent power potential, the upside just isn’t there to draft Morse in any but the deepest of leagues.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The Pirates have a lot of options at first base.  The John Jaso-Michael Morse platoon may be the most likely, at least to start the season, but that risk and Morse’s declining production make him unattractive in most fantasy formats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        
    </tr>
</table>
